<p><b>Benjamin Solomon Carson Sr.</b> (born September 18, 1951) is an American retired neurosurgeon, author, and politician, and nominee to be the 17th <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Housing_and_Urban_Development" title="United States Secretary of Housing and Urban Development">United States Secretary of Housing and Urban Development</a>.</p>
<p>Born in <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>, and a graduate of <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Health_System" title="University of Michigan Health System">University of Michigan Medical School</a>, Carson has authored numerous books on his medical career and political stances. He was the subject of a <a href="/wiki/Gifted_Hands:_The_Ben_Carson_Story" title="Gifted Hands: The Ben Carson Story">television drama film</a> in 2009.</p>
<p>He was the Director of Pediatric Neurosurgery at <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_Hospital" title="Johns Hopkins Hospital">Johns Hopkins Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a> from 1984 until his retirement in 2013. As a pioneer in neurosurgery, Carson's achievements include performing the only successful separation of <a href="/wiki/Conjoined_twins" title="Conjoined twins">conjoined twins</a> joined at the back of the head, pioneering the first successful neurosurgical procedure on a fetus inside the womb, performing the first completely successful separation of type-2 vertical <a href="/wiki/Craniopagus_twins" title="Craniopagus twins">craniopagus twins</a>, developing new methods to treat brain-stem tumors, and reviving <a href="/wiki/Hemispherectomy" title="Hemispherectomy">hemispherectomy</a> techniques for controlling <a href="/wiki/Epileptic_seizure" title="Epileptic seizure">seizures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> He became the youngest chief of pediatric neurosurgery in the country at age 33.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> He has received more than 60 honorary doctorate degrees, dozens of national merit citations, and written over 100 neurosurgical publications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> In 2008, he was bestowed the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a>, the highest civilian award in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carson's widely publicized speech at the 2013 <a href="/wiki/National_Prayer_Breakfast" title="National Prayer Breakfast">National Prayer Breakfast</a> catapulted him to <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservative</a> fame for his views on social and political issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> On May 4, 2015, he announced he was running for the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016" title="Republican Party presidential primaries, 2016">Republican nomination</a> in the 2016 presidential election at a rally in his hometown of Detroit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Politico_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Politico-14">[14]</a></sup> In March 2016, following the <a href="/wiki/Super_Tuesday" title="Super Tuesday">Super Tuesday</a> primaries, he suspended his campaign and announced he would be the new national chairman of My Faith Votes, a group that encourages Christians to exercise their civic duty to vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTerrisWashPst03022016_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-BTerrisWashPst03022016-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TKopanCNN03042016_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-TKopanCNN03042016-16">[16]</a></sup> He then endorsed the candidacy of <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-washingtonpost.com_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-washingtonpost.com-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ben_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Carson was born in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detroit,_Michigan" title="Detroit, Michigan">Detroit, Michigan</a>, the son of Sonya (Johnnie) (née Copeland) and Robert Solomon Carson, Jr. (December 27, 1914 – August 29, 1992), a World War II U.S. Army veteran, then a Baptist minister, and later a <a href="/wiki/Cadillac" title="Cadillac">Cadillac</a> automobile plant laborer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Both parents came from large families in rural Georgia and were living in rural Tennessee when they met and married.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gates_2009_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gates_2009-19">[19]</a></sup> Carson's mother was 13 and his father was 28 when they married, and after his father finished his military service, they moved from <a href="/wiki/Chattanooga,_Tennessee" title="Chattanooga, Tennessee">Chattanooga, Tennessee</a> to Detroit,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gates_2009_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gates_2009-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990_p19_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990_p19-20">[20]</a></sup> where they lived in a large house in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Village,_Detroit" title="Indian Village, Detroit">Indian Village</a> neighborhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Carson's older brother, Curtis, was born in 1949, when his mother was 20.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In 1950, Carson’s parents purchased a new 733-square foot single-family detached home on Deacon Street in the <a href="/wiki/Boynton%E2%80%93Oakwood_Heights_(Detroit)" title="Boynton–Oakwood Heights (Detroit)">Boynton</a> neighborhood in southwest Detroit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glover_2015_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glover_2015-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carson's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detroit_Public_Schools" title="Detroit Public Schools">Detroit Public Schools</a> education began in 1956 with kindergarten at the Fisher School, and continued through first, second, and the first half of third grade, during which time he was an average student.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2012_p53_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2012_p53-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Detroit_Board_of_Education_1972_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Detroit_Board_of_Education_1972-27">[27]</a></sup> When Carson was five, his mother learned that his father had a prior family and had not divorced his first wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1992_pp31.E2.80.9355_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1992_pp31.E2.80.9355-28">[28]</a></sup> In 1959, when Carson was eight, his parents separated and he moved with mother and brother to live for two years with his mother’s <a href="/wiki/Seventh-day_Adventist_Church" title="Seventh-day Adventist Church">Seventh-day Adventist</a> older sister and her sister’s husband in multi-family dwellings in the <a href="/wiki/Dorchester,_Boston" title="Dorchester, Boston">Dorchester</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roxbury,_Boston" title="Roxbury, Boston">Roxbury</a> neighborhoods of <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1992_pp31.E2.80.9355_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1992_pp31.E2.80.9355-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_2015_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson_2015-29">[29]</a></sup> In Boston, Carson’s mother attempted suicide, had several psychiatric hospitalizations for depression, and for the first time began working outside the home as a <a href="/wiki/Domestic_worker" title="Domestic worker">domestic worker</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1992_pp31.E2.80.9355_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1992_pp31.E2.80.9355-28">[28]</a></sup> while Carson and his brother attended a two-classroom school at the Berea Seventh-day Adventist church where two teachers taught eight grades and the vast majority of time was spent singing songs and playing games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2012_p53_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2012_p53-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1961, when Carson was ten, he moved with his mother and brother back to southwest Detroit, where they lived in a multi-family dwelling in a primarily white neighborhood (<a href="/wiki/Springwells_Village" title="Springwells Village">Springwells Village</a>) across the railroad tracks from the <a href="/wiki/Delray,_Detroit" title="Delray, Detroit">Delray</a> neighborhood, while renting out their house on Deacon Street which his mother received in a divorce settlement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glover_2015_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glover_2015-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1992_pp31.E2.80.9355_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1992_pp31.E2.80.9355-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2014_p48_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2014_p48-30">[30]</a></sup> When they returned to Detroit public schools, Carson and his brother’s academic performance initially lagged far behind their new classmates, having essentially lost a year of school by attending a Seventh-day Adventist church school in Boston,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2012_p53_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2012_p53-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1992_pp31.E2.80.9355_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1992_pp31.E2.80.9355-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1992_p15_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1992_p15-31">[31]</a></sup> but both improved when their mother limited their time watching television and required them to read and write book reports on two library books per week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990_pp35.E2.80.9336_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990_pp35.E2.80.9336-32">[32]</a></sup> Carson attended the predominantly white Higgins Elementary School for fifth and sixth grades and the predominantly white Wilson Junior High School for seventh and the first half of eighth grade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Detroit_Board_of_Education_1972_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Detroit_Board_of_Education_1972-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990_pp29.2C_40_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990_pp29.2C_40-33">[33]</a></sup> In 1965, when Carson was thirteen, he moved with his mother and brother back to their house on Deacon Street.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990_pp46.E2.80.9347_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990_pp46.E2.80.9347-34">[34]</a></sup> He attended the predominantly black Hunter Junior High School for the second half of eighth grade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Detroit_Board_of_Education_1972_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Detroit_Board_of_Education_1972-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990_pp46.E2.80.9347_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990_pp46.E2.80.9347-34">[34]</a></sup> When he was eight, Carson had dreamed of becoming a missionary doctor, but five years later he aspired to the lucrative lifestyles of <a href="/wiki/Psychiatrist" title="Psychiatrist">psychiatrists</a> portrayed on television, and his brother bought him a subscription to <i><a href="/wiki/Psychology_Today" title="Psychology Today">Psychology Today</a></i> for his thirteenth birthday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990_p62_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990_p62-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1999_p46_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1999_p46-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>By ninth grade, the family's financial situation had improved, with his mother surprising neighbors by paying cash to buy a new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chrysler_(division)" title="Chrysler (division)">Chrysler</a> car,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1992_pp179_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1992_pp179-37">[37]</a></sup> and the only government assistance they still relied on was <a href="/wiki/Supplemental_Nutrition_Assistance_Program" title="Supplemental Nutrition Assistance Program">food stamps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p48.2C_49.E2.80.9350_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p48.2C_49.E2.80.9350-38">[38]</a></sup> Carson attended the predominantly black Southwestern High School for ninth through twelfth grades, graduating ranked third in his class academically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Detroit_Board_of_Education_1972_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-Detroit_Board_of_Education_1972-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990.2C_2011_pp69.2C_72.2C_73.2C_74_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990.2C_2011_pp69.2C_72.2C_73.2C_74-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2014_Beyond_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2014_Beyond-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2015_p79_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2015_p79-41">[41]</a></sup> In high school he played the baritone horn in the band, and participated in forensics (public speaking),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2015_pp61.E2.80.9364_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2015_pp61.E2.80.9364-42">[42]</a></sup> chess club,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2014_p35_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2014_p35-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2015a_pp46.E2.80.9347_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2015a_pp46.E2.80.9347-44">[44]</a></sup> and the U.S. Army <a href="/wiki/Junior_Reserve_Officers%27_Training_Corps" title="Junior Reserve Officers' Training Corps">Junior Reserve Officers' Training Corps</a> (JROTC) program where he reached its highest rank—cadet colonel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990.2C_2011_pp69.2C_72.2C_73.2C_74_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990.2C_2011_pp69.2C_72.2C_73.2C_74-39">[39]</a></sup> Carson served as a laboratory assistant in the high school's biology, chemistry, physics school laboratories beginning in tenth, eleventh, and twelfth grade, respectively,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2015_pp56.E2.80.9357_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2015_pp56.E2.80.9357-45">[45]</a></sup> and worked as a biology laboratory assistant at <a href="/wiki/Wayne_State_University" title="Wayne State University">Wayne State University</a> the summer between eleventh and twelfth grades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2015_pp66.E2.80.9367_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2015_pp66.E2.80.9367-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carson’s <a href="/wiki/SAT" title="SAT">SAT</a> college admission test scores ranked him somewhere in the low ninetieth percentile, which according to him resulted in a <i><a href="/wiki/Detroit_Free_Press" title="Detroit Free Press">Detroit Free Press</a></i> article "Carson Gets Highest SAT Scores in Twenty Years" of any student in Detroit public schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2014_Beyond_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2014_Beyond-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2015_p79_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2015_p79-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p70_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p70-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1999_p182_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1999_p182-48">[48]</a></sup> He wanted to attend college farther away than his brother who was at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p79_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p79-49">[49]</a></sup> Carson says he narrowed his college choices to <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard</a> or <a href="/wiki/Yale_College" title="Yale College">Yale</a>, but could only afford the $10 application fee to apply to one of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2015_p79_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2015_p79-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p73_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p73-50">[50]</a></sup> He said he decided to apply to Yale after seeing a team from Yale defeat a team from Harvard on the <i><a href="/wiki/College_Bowl" title="College Bowl">G.E. College Bowl</a></i> television show.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p74_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p74-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1999_p200_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1999_p200-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2008_p82_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2008_p82-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2015_p79.E2.80.9380_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2015_p79.E2.80.9380-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2015b_p87_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2015b_p87-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-College_Bowl_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-College_Bowl-56">[56]</a></sup> Carson was accepted by Yale and offered a full scholarship covering tuition, room and board.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p74.2C_82_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p74.2C_82-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2015b_p41_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2015b_p41-58">[58]</a></sup> Carson graduated with a B.A. in psychology from Yale in 1973 "with a fairly respectable grade point average, although far from the top of the class."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p103_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p103-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2013_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2013-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carson does not say in his books whether he applied for and received a <a href="/wiki/Selective_Service_System" title="Selective Service System">college student deferment</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>. He does say that his older brother, who was a student at the University of Michigan, received a low number (26) in the <a href="/wiki/Draft_lottery_(1969)" title="Draft lottery (1969)">first draft lottery</a> in 1969 and enlisted in the Navy for four years instead of being drafted,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> whereas he received a high number (333) in the second draft lottery in 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2012_p130_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2012_p130-62">[62]</a></sup> Carson said he would have readily accepted his responsibility to fight had he been drafted,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2012_p130_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2012_p130-62">[62]</a></sup> but he "identified strongly with the antiwar protesters and the revolutionaries" and enthusiastically voted for antiwar Democratic presidential candidate <a href="/wiki/George_McGovern" title="George McGovern">George McGovern</a> in 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> In his book, <i>America the Beautiful</i> (2012), Carson said: "The Vietnam War was, in retrospect, not a noble conflict. It brought shame to our nation because of both the outcome and the cause".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2012_p127_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2012_p127-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the summers following his high school graduation until before his second year in medical school, Carson worked at variety of jobs: as a clerk in the payroll office of <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a>, as a supervisor of a six-person crew picking up trash along the highway that was part of a federal government jobs program for inner-city students, as a clerk in the mailroom of <a href="/wiki/Young_%26_Rubicam" title="Young &amp; Rubicam">Young &amp; Rubicam Advertising</a>, assembling fender parts and inspecting back window louvers on the assembly line at <a href="/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler</a>, as a crane operator at Sennett Steel, and finally as a radiology technician taking X-rays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990.2C_2011_pp82.E2.80.9390.2C_104.E2.80.93107_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990.2C_2011_pp82.E2.80.9390.2C_104.E2.80.93107-65">[65]</a></sup> At Yale, Carson had a part-time job on campus as a student police aide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2016_pp21.E2.80.9322_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2016_pp21.E2.80.9322-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carson entered the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Health_System" title="University of Michigan Health System">University of Michigan Medical School</a> in 1973, and at first struggled academically, doing so poorly on his first set of comprehensive exams that his faculty adviser recommended that he drop out of medical school or take a reduced academic load and take longer to finish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2008_p94_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2008_p94-67">[67]</a></sup> He continued with a regular academic load, and his grades improved to average in his first year of medical school, and he began to excel in his second year of medical by seldom attending lectures, but studying textbooks and lecture notes from 6 a.m. to 11 p.m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p107_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p107-68">[68]</a></sup> Carson graduated from the University of Michigan Medical School with an M.D. in 1977, and was elected to the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Omega_Alpha" title="Alpha Omega Alpha">Alpha Omega Alpha</a> Honor Medical Society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_2013_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_2013-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carson was then accepted by the <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_School_of_Medicine" title="Johns Hopkins School of Medicine">Johns Hopkins University School of Medicine</a> neurosurgery program, where he served one year as a surgical intern and five years as a neurosurgery <a href="/wiki/Residency_(medicine)" title="Residency (medicine)">resident</a>, completing the final year as chief resident in 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p125.E2.80.93126_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p125.E2.80.93126-69">[69]</a></sup> He then spent one year (1983–1984) as a <a href="/wiki/Senior_registrar" title="Senior registrar">Senior Registrar</a> in neurosurgery at the <a href="/wiki/Sir_Charles_Gairdner_Hospital" title="Sir Charles Gairdner Hospital">Sir Charles Gairdner Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nedlands,_Western_Australia" title="Nedlands, Western Australia">Nedlands</a>, a suburb of <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p135.E2.80.93136_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson_1990.2C_2011_p135.E2.80.93136-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his book <i>Gifted Hands</i>, Carson relates that, in his youth, he had a violent temper. "As a teenager, I would go after people with rocks, and bricks, and baseball bats, and hammers," Carson told NBC's <i><a href="/wiki/Meet_the_Press" title="Meet the Press">Meet the Press</a></i> in October 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> He said he once tried to hit his mother over the head with a hammer over a clothing dispute and, while in the ninth grade, he attempted to stab a friend who had changed the station on the radio; the blade broke in his friend's belt buckle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTwestpoint_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTwestpoint-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fritze_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fritze-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biography.yourdictionary.com_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-biography.yourdictionary.com-74">[74]</a></sup> He said that the intended victim, whose identity he wants to protect, was a classmate, a friend, or a close relative in different versions of the story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malaysia_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Malaysia-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> After this incident, Carson said that he began reading the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Proverbs" title="Book of Proverbs">Book of Proverbs</a> and applying verses on anger. As a result, he states he "never had another problem with temper".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-achievement.org_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-achievement.org-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ben_Carson.2C_M.D._50.E2.80.9353_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ben_Carson.2C_M.D._50.E2.80.9353-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dr._Ben_Carson_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dr._Ben_Carson-79">[79]</a></sup> In his various books and at campaign events, he repeated these claims and said he once attacked a schoolmate with a <a href="/wiki/Combination_lock" title="Combination lock">combination lock</a>. But nine friends, classmates, and neighbors who grew up with him told CNN in 2015 they did not remember the anger or violence he has described; all expressed surprise about the incidents Carson said had happened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tale_of_two_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-tale_of_two-80">[80]</a></sup> In response, Carson posted on Facebook what he said was a 1997 <i>Parade Magazine</i> issue, in which his mother verified the stabbing incident. He then questioned the extent of the effort CNN had exerted in the investigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malaysia_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Malaysia-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>He has said that he protected white students in a biology lab after a <a href="/wiki/1968_Detroit_riot" title="1968 Detroit riot">race riot</a> broke out at his high school in response to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." title="Assassination of Martin Luther King, Jr.">assassination of Martin Luther King, Jr.</a> in 1968. The <i>Wall Street Journal</i> confirmed the riot but could not find anyone who remembered Carson sheltering white students. Reportedly, Carson could also not remember the names of any of the students he claimed he had helped<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJ_deeper_questions_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ_deeper_questions-81">[81]</a></sup> but has said perhaps they will verify the incident, eventually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his autobiography, Carson claimed to have been offered scholarships to both the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">West Point</a>, though the latter one was disputed during his 2016 presidential run. On November 6, 2015, <i>Politico</i> reported a story challenging that narrative: West Point has no record of his ever seeking admission. Furthermore, the academy does not award scholarships to anyone;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witch_hunt_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-witch_hunt-83">[83]</a></sup> cadets receive a free education and room and board in exchange for a commitment to serve in the military for at least five years after graduation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witch_hunt_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-witch_hunt-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the same autobiography, Carson claimed he had dinner with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_William_Westmoreland" title="General William Westmoreland">General William Westmoreland</a> on Memorial Day in Detroit in 1969. Pentagon records, however, show that Westmoreland was not in Detroit on that date or the preceding or following days. According to those records, Westmoreland was in Washington, D.C. on the evening in question, engaged in a "boat ride on the Potomac".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>In one book, Carson also said the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a> had offered him a scholarship. However, his staff later said that the described scenario was similar to that of West Point, as he never actually applied for entry to the University of Michigan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his autobiography, <i>Gifted Hands</i>, Carson recounted that exams for a Yale psychology course he took his junior year, "Perceptions 301", were inexplicably burned, forcing students to retake the exam. Carson claimed that other students walked out in protest when they discovered the retest was significantly harder than the original examination, but he alone finished the test. On doing so, Carson claimed he was congratulated by the course instructor who told him the retest was a hoax intended to find "the most honest student in the class". Carson said the professor awarded him $10, and further claimed that a photographer for the <i><a href="/wiki/Yale_Daily_News" title="Yale Daily News">Yale Daily News</a></i> was present to take his picture, which appeared in the student newspaper with a story about the experiment. Doubts were raised about this story in 2015 during Carson's presidential campaign. <i>The Wall Street Journal</i> attempted to verify Carson's account, reporting that Yale undergraduate courses were identified with only two digits in the early 1970s, and that Yale offered no course called "Perceptions 301" at that time. Carson's photo never appeared in the <i>Yale Daily News</i>, the <i>Journal</i> reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJ_deeper_questions_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ_deeper_questions-81">[81]</a></sup> Carson, while acknowledging the class number was not correct, said: "You know, when you write a book with a co-writer and you say that there was a class, a lot of [the] time they’ll put a number or something just to give it more meat. You know, obviously, decades later, I’m not going to remember the course number."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abcn_.27Thi_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-abcn_.27Thi-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>On Facebook, Carson posted a link to a syllabus titled "Perception" on Yale's website, used for the course "Psychology 323b" offered in Spring 2002, three decades after the incident described in Carson's book. Carson stated that he was waiting for an apology from <i>The Wall Street Journal</i> and questioned how they, with all their investigative capacities, were unable to find the syllabus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> To further bolster the veracity of his <i>Gifted Hands</i> account, Carson also posted to Facebook a <i>Yale Daily News</i> article published January 14, 1970, during his freshman year. The article described a parody issue of the <i>Yale Daily News</i> which had been created and distributed the day before by campus humor magazine <i><a href="/wiki/The_Yale_Record" title="The Yale Record">The Yale Record</a></i>. The parody issue had included an announcement that exams for Psychology 10 had been destroyed, and students were required to take a make-up exam that evening in a classroom named in the article.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson9Nov_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson9Nov-89">[89]</a></sup> According to the <i>Yale Daily News</i>, a number of students were fooled by the humor magazine's prank and showed up to take the test, which was said to closely resemble the course's real exam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson9Nov_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson9Nov-89">[89]</a></sup> Carson was not named in the 1970 <i>Yale Daily News</i> article, his photograph was not published, and no faculty member was reported to have cooperated with the prank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson9Nov_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson9Nov-89">[89]</a></sup> In 2015, several Yale alumni who remembered the 1970 prank came forward, verifying that a student who worked for the <i>Yale Record</i> had impersonated an exam proctor, and that small cash awards were indeed given to students who finished the hoax exam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson9NovB_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson9NovB-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Surgeon">Surgeon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ben_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Surgeon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="Unbalanced scales.svg" data-file-height="354" data-file-width="400" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/45px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/68px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/90px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 2x" width="45"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>may be <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view#Undue_weight" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">unbalanced</a> towards certain viewpoints</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ben_Carson&amp;action=edit">improve the article</a> by adding information on neglected viewpoints, or discuss the issue on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Ben_Carson" title="Talk:Ben Carson">talk page</a>.</span> <small><i>(November 2015)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ben_Carson.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="139" data-file-width="100" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7a/Ben_Carson.jpg" width="100"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ben_Carson.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carson during his career as a surgeon</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After medical school, Carson completed his residency in neurosurgery at <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_Hospital" title="Johns Hopkins Hospital">Johns Hopkins Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Around this time, as Carson later related to Karen Hunter of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sirius_XM" title="Sirius XM">Sirius XM</a>, he was held at gunpoint at a <a href="/wiki/Popeyes_Louisiana_Kitchen" title="Popeyes Louisiana Kitchen">Popeyes</a> restaurant in Baltimore. <a href="/wiki/Armstrong_Williams" title="Armstrong Williams">Armstrong Williams</a>, Carson's campaign business manager, later told <a href="/wiki/Wolf_Blitzer" title="Wolf Blitzer">Wolf Blitzer</a> of CNN that several people in the neighborhood chased the robber down the street. Neither the Baltimore police department nor Popeyes could corroborate Carson's story, since no police report had been found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1983, at the suggestion of an Australian colleague, he accepted the position of senior registrar at <a href="/wiki/Sir_Charles_Gairdner_Hospital" title="Sir Charles Gairdner Hospital">Sir Charles Gairdner Hospital</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>), spending one year there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perth_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-perth-93">[93]</a></sup> Upon returning to Johns Hopkins in 1984, Carson was appointed the university's Director of Pediatric Neurosurgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> As a surgeon, he specialized in traumatic brain injuries, brain and spinal cord tumors, <a href="/wiki/Achondroplasia" title="Achondroplasia">achondroplasia</a>, neurological and congenital disorders, <a href="/wiki/Craniosynostosis" title="Craniosynostosis">craniosynostosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Trigeminal_neuralgia" title="Trigeminal neuralgia">trigeminal neuralgia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-95">[95]</a></sup> He has said that his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hand%E2%80%93eye_coordination" title="Hand–eye coordination">hand–eye coordination</a> and three-dimensional reasoning made him a gifted surgeon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PennState_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-PennState-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>While at Johns Hopkins, Carson figured in the revival of the <a href="/wiki/Hemispherectomy" title="Hemispherectomy">hemispherectomy</a>, a drastic surgical procedure in which part or all of one hemisphere of the brain is removed to control severe pediatric <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a>. Encouraged by <a href="/wiki/John_M._Freeman" title="John M. Freeman">John M. Freeman</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> he refined the procedure in the 1980s and performed it many times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1987, Carson was the lead neurosurgeon of a 70-member surgical team that separated <a href="/wiki/Conjoined_twins" title="Conjoined twins">conjoined twins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_and_Benjamin_Binder" title="Patrick and Benjamin Binder">Patrick and Benjamin Binder</a>, who had been joined at the back of the head (<a href="/wiki/Craniopagus_twins" title="Craniopagus twins">craniopagus twins</a>); the separation surgery held promise in part because the twin boys had separate brains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terris13Nov_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Terris13Nov-100">[100]</a></sup> Both boys entered the hospital "giggling and kicking" in preparation for surgery without which, it was said at the time, the seven-month-old twins would never have been able to crawl, walk, or turn over.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terris13Nov_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Terris13Nov-100">[100]</a></sup> The Johns Hopkins surgical team rehearsed the surgery for weeks, practising on two dolls secured together by <a href="/wiki/Velcro" title="Velcro">Velcro</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terris13Nov_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-Terris13Nov-100">[100]</a></sup> Although follow-up stories were few following the Binder twins' return to Germany seven months after the operation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terris13Nov_100-3"><a href="#cite_note-Terris13Nov-100">[100]</a></sup> both twins were reportedly "far from normal" two years after the procedure, with one in a <a href="/wiki/Persistent_vegetative_state" title="Persistent vegetative state">vegetative state</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terris13Nov_100-4"><a href="#cite_note-Terris13Nov-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> "I will never get over this . . . Why did I have them separated?" said their mother, Theresia Binder, in a 1993 interview.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terris13Nov_100-5"><a href="#cite_note-Terris13Nov-100">[100]</a></sup> Neither twin was ever able to talk or care for himself, and both would eventually become institutionalized <a href="/wiki/Ward_(law)" title="Ward (law)">wards of the state</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terris13Nov_100-6"><a href="#cite_note-Terris13Nov-100">[100]</a></sup> Patrick Binder died sometime during the last decade, according to his uncle, who was located by the <i>Washington Post</i> in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terris13Nov_100-7"><a href="#cite_note-Terris13Nov-100">[100]</a></sup> The Binder surgery served as blueprint for similar twin separations, a procedure which was refined in subsequent decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terris13Nov_100-8"><a href="#cite_note-Terris13Nov-100">[100]</a></sup> Carson participated in four subsequent high-risk conjoined twin separations, including a 1997 operation on craniopagus <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambian</a> twins, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_and_Luka_Banda" title="Joseph and Luka Banda">Joseph and Luka Banda</a>, which resulted in a normal neurological outcome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terris13Nov_100-9"><a href="#cite_note-Terris13Nov-100">[100]</a></sup> Two sets of twins died, including <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iranian</a> twins <a href="/wiki/Ladan_and_Laleh_Bijani" title="Ladan and Laleh Bijani">Ladan and Laleh Bijani</a>; another separation resulted in the death of one twin and the survival of another, who is legally blind and struggles to walk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belluck22Nov_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belluck22Nov-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <i>Washington Post</i>, the Binder surgery "launched the stardom" of Carson, who "walked out of the operating room that day into a spotlight that has never dimmed", beginning with a press conference that was covered worldwide, which created name recognition ultimately leading to publishing deals and a <a href="/wiki/Motivational_speaker" title="Motivational speaker">motivational speaking</a> career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terris13Nov_100-10"><a href="#cite_note-Terris13Nov-100">[100]</a></sup> On the condition the film would have its premiere in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltimore,_Maryland" title="Baltimore, Maryland">Baltimore</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terris13Nov_100-11"><a href="#cite_note-Terris13Nov-100">[100]</a></sup> Carson agreed to a cameo appearance as "head surgeon" in the 2003 <a href="/wiki/Farrelly_brothers" title="Farrelly brothers">Farrelly brothers</a>' comedy <i><a href="/wiki/Stuck_on_You_(film)" title="Stuck on You (film)">Stuck on You</a></i>, starring <a href="/wiki/Matt_Damon" title="Matt Damon">Matt Damon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greg_Kinnear" title="Greg Kinnear">Greg Kinnear</a> as conjoined twins who, unhappy after their surgical separation, continue life attached to each other by Velcro.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terris13Nov_100-12"><a href="#cite_note-Terris13Nov-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griswold13Aug_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Griswold13Aug-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2013, Carson announced he would retire as a surgeon, saying: "I'd much rather quit when I'm at the top of my game."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> His retirement became official on July 1, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Articles.2C_books.2C_business_relationships.2C_media_posts">Articles, books, business relationships, media posts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ben_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Articles, books, business relationships, media posts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Carson has written many articles in peer-reviewed journals<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Interview_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Interview-108">[108]</a></sup> and six <a href="/wiki/Bestseller" title="Bestseller">bestselling</a> books<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> published by <a href="/wiki/Zondervan" title="Zondervan">Zondervan</a>, an international Christian media and publishing company. The first book was an <a href="/wiki/Autobiography" title="Autobiography">autobiography</a> published in 1992. Two others are about his personal philosophies of success, which include focused, high-quality work and what he sees as the stabilizing influence of religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>On August 7, 2002, Carson underwent surgery for <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">prostate cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BaltSun1_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-BaltSun1-112">[112]</a></sup> Interviewed in the following November, he said the surgery had successfully removed all cancerous tissue and he was completely cured of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-700Club_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-700Club-113">[113]</a></sup> In 2004, in a speech at a <a href="/wiki/Mannatech" title="Mannatech">Mannatech</a>, Inc. event, he credited the company's products with the disappearance of his cancer symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GrabowMannatech_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-GrabowMannatech-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaremontMannatech_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-MaremontMannatech-115">[115]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a>, Carson had an "extensive relationship" from 2004 to 2014 with Mannatech, a <a href="/wiki/Multi-level_marketing" title="Multi-level marketing">multi-level marketing</a> company that produces <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplement" title="Dietary supplement">dietary supplements</a> made from substances such as <a href="/wiki/Aloe_vera" title="Aloe vera">aloe vera</a> extract and <a href="/wiki/Larch" title="Larch">larch-tree</a> bark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GrabowMannatech_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-GrabowMannatech-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaremontMannatech_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-MaremontMannatech-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PF102915_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-PF102915-116">[116]</a></sup> Carson gave four paid speeches at company events. He has denied being paid by Mannatech to do anything else, saying he has been a "prolific speaker" who has addressed many groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> The nature of this relationship became an issue in 2015 during Carson's presidential campaign. Carson's relationship with Mannatech continued after the company paid $7 million in 2009 to settle a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deceptive_marketing" title="Deceptive marketing">deceptive-marketing</a> lawsuit, in Texas, over claims that its products could cure <a href="/wiki/Autism" title="Autism">autism</a> and cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GrabowMannatech_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-GrabowMannatech-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> His most recent paid speech for the company was in 2013, for which he was paid $42,000. His image appeared on the corporation's website in 2014,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GrabowMannatech_114-3"><a href="#cite_note-GrabowMannatech-114">[114]</a></sup> and in the same year he praised their "glyconutrient" supplements in a <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a> special that was subsequently featured on the site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geraghty2015_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geraghty2015-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carson delivered the keynote address at a Mannatech distributor convention in 2011, during which he said the company had donated funds to help him obtain a coveted endowed chair post at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_Medicine" title="Johns Hopkins Medicine">Johns Hopkins Medicine</a>: "... three years ago I had an endowed chair bestowed upon me and uh, it requires $2.5 million to do an endowed chair and I'm proud to say that part of that $2.5 million came from Mannatech." In October 2015, Carson's campaign team said "there was no contribution from Mannatech to Johns Hopkins", and his statement had been "a legitimate mistake on his part. Confusion. He had been doing some fundraising for the hospital and some other chairs about that time, and he simply got things mixed up."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJ_JH_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ_JH-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/CNBC" title="CNBC">CNBC</a> GOP debate on October 28, 2015, Carson was asked about his relationship with Mannatech. He replied, "That's easy to answer. I didn't have any involvement with Mannatech. Total propaganda. I did a couple speeches for them. I did speeches for other people—they were paid speeches. It is absolutely absurd to say I had any kind of relation with them. Do I take the product? Yes. I think it is a good product."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ford-122">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/PolitiFact.com" title="PolitiFact.com">Politifact</a> rated Carson's denial of any involvement as "false", pointing to his paid speeches for Mannatech and his appearances in promotional videos in which he favorably reviewed its products, despite not being "an official spokesman or sales associate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PF102915_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-PF102915-116">[116]</a></sup> When the CNBC moderator commented that Carson was on Mannatech's website, Carson replied that he had not given his permission. Earlier, he had said that he was unaware of the company's legal history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Review_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-National_Review-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 3, 2015, Mannatech said on its website that for compliance with <a href="/wiki/Campaign_finance_in_the_United_States" title="Campaign finance in the United States">Federal campaign finance regulations</a> the company had removed all references to Carson before he announced his bid for the presidency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mannatech_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mannatech-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2013, Carson was hired by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Times" title="The Washington Times">The Washington Times</a></i> as a weekly opinion columnist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> In October 2013, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fox_News_Channel" title="Fox News Channel">Fox News</a> hired Carson as a contributor, to provide analysis and commentary across Fox News Channel's daytime and primetime programming, a relationship which lasted to the end of 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>In financial disclosure forms, Carson and his wife reported income of between $8.9 million and $27 million from January 2014 to May 3, 2015, when he announced his presidential campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FloresIncome_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FloresIncome-127">[127]</a></sup> Over that period, Carson received over $4 million from 141 paid speeches; between $1.1 million and $6 million in book <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royalties" title="Royalties">royalties</a>; between $200,000 to $2 million as a contributor to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_Times" title="Washington Times">Washington Times</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Fox_News" title="Fox News">Fox News</a>; and between $2 million and $10 million as a member of the boards of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kellogg_Co." title="Kellogg Co.">Kellogg Co.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Costco" title="Costco">Costco</a> Wholesale Corp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FloresIncome_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-FloresIncome-127">[127]</a></sup> He resigned from Costco's board in mid-2015, after serving on it for more than 16 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boards_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boards-128">[128]</a></sup> Carson was Chairman of the Baltimore-based <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> company <a href="/wiki/Vaccinogen" title="Vaccinogen">Vaccinogen</a> from August 2014<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> until the announcement of his US presidential bid in May 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Carson had previously served on Vaccinogen's Medical Advisory Board.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="2016_presidential_campaign">2016 presidential campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ben_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 2016 presidential campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ben_Carson_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Ben Carson presidential campaign, 2016">Ben Carson presidential campaign, 2016</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg/220px-Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg/330px-Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg/440px-Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carson speaking at a campaign event in August 2015</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background_and_increasing_political_visibility">Background and increasing political visibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ben_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Background and increasing political visibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carson, who had been registered as a <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a>, changed his registration to <a href="/wiki/Independent_politician" title="Independent politician">independent</a> in the 1990s after watching Republicans <a href="/wiki/Impeachment_of_Bill_Clinton" title="Impeachment of Bill Clinton">impeach President Clinton</a> for perjury and obstruction of justice regarding an <a href="/wiki/Lewinsky_scandal" title="Lewinsky scandal">extramarital affair</a> with <a href="/wiki/Monica_Lewinsky" title="Monica Lewinsky">Monica Lewinsky</a>. "I just saw so much hypocrisy in both parties", he said.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Switches_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Switches-132">[132]</a></sup> In February 2013, Carson said he was not a member of any <a href="/wiki/Political_party" title="Political party">political party</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NR_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-NR-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his book <i>America the Beautiful</i> (2013), he wrote: "I believe it is a very good idea for physicians, scientists, engineers, and others trained to make decisions based on facts and empirical data to get involved in the political arena."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carson was the keynote speaker at the <a href="/wiki/National_Prayer_Breakfast" title="National Prayer Breakfast">National Prayer Breakfast</a> on February 7, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> The speech garnered Carson considerable attention because the event is normally apolitical in nature, and the speech was critical of the philosophy and policies of President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>, who was sitting 10 feet away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>About the speech, Carson said: "I don't think it was particularly political... You know, I'm a physician".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rea_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rea-138">[138]</a></sup> Regarding the policies of President Obama, he said: "There are a number of policies that I don't believe lead to the growth of our nation and don't lead to the elevation of our nation. I don't want to sit here and say all of his policies are bad. What I would like to see more often in this nation is an open and intelligent conversation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rea_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rea-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carson's sudden popularity among conservatives led to his being invited as a featured speaker at the 2013 <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Political_Action_Conference" title="Conservative Political Action Conference">Conservative Political Action Conference</a> (CPAC). He tied for seventh place in the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Times" title="The Washington Times">Washington Times</a></i>/CPAC 2013 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Straw_Poll" title="Straw Poll">Straw Poll</a> with 4% of the 3,000 ballots cast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> In the 2014 CPAC straw poll, he was in third place with 9% of the vote, behind <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">senators</a> <a href="/wiki/Ted_Cruz" title="Ted Cruz">Ted Cruz</a> of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> (with 11%) and <a href="/wiki/Rand_Paul" title="Rand Paul">Rand Paul</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a> (31%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> In the presidential straw poll at the 2013 <a href="/wiki/Values_Voter_Summit" title="Values Voter Summit">Values Voter Summit</a> he and <a href="/wiki/Rick_Santorum" title="Rick Santorum">Rick Santorum</a> polled 13%, with winner Ted Cruz polling 42%, and in 2014 he polled 20% to Cruz's winning 25%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 4, 2014, the day of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_midterms" title="2014 midterms">2014 midterms</a>, he rejoined the Republican Party, saying it was "truly a pragmatic move" because he was considering running for <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2016" title="United States presidential election, 2016">president in 2016</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Switches_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Switches-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 2015, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Weekly_Standard" title="The Weekly Standard">The Weekly Standard</a></i> reported that the Draft Carson Committee had raised $13 million by the end of 2014, shortly after Carson performed well in a <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a>/<a href="/wiki/ORC_International" title="ORC International">ORC</a> poll of potential candidates in December 2014, coming second in two different versions. He polled 10% to <a href="/wiki/Mitt_Romney" title="Mitt Romney">Mitt Romney</a>'s 20%, but in the same poll with Romney removed from the list, Carson polled 11% to <a href="/wiki/Jeb_Bush" title="Jeb Bush">Jeb Bush</a>'s 14%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes1_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes1-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Journal" title="Wall Street Journal">Wall Street Journal</a></i> mentioned that the Draft Carson Committee had chairmen in all of <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>'s <a href="/wiki/List_of_counties_in_Iowa" title="List of counties in Iowa">99 counties</a>, and that Carson had recently led two separate <a href="/wiki/Public_Policy_Polling" title="Public Policy Polling">Public Policy polls</a> for the state of <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Announcement_of_campaign">Announcement of campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ben_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Announcement of campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On May 2, 2015, Carson proclaimed that in two days, he was going to make a major announcement on his decision on whether to enter the Presidential Race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> In an interview with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cincinnati,_Ohio" title="Cincinnati, Ohio">Cincinnati</a> TV station <a href="/wiki/WKRC_(AM)" title="WKRC (AM)">WKRC (AM)</a> on May 3, 2015, Carson accidentally confirmed his candidacy for president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> The interview was also broadcast live on <a href="/wiki/WPEC" title="WPEC">WPEC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> The next day, May 4, 2015, at the <a href="/wiki/Music_Hall_Center_for_the_Performing_Arts" title="Music Hall Center for the Performing Arts">Music Hall Center for the Performing Arts</a> in his home town of <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Politico_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Politico-14">[14]</a></sup> he officially announced his run for the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016" title="Republican Party presidential primaries, 2016">Republican nomination</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2016" title="United States presidential election, 2016">2016 U.S. presidential election</a>. The announcement speech was preceded by a choir singing "<a href="/wiki/Lose_Yourself" title="Lose Yourself">Lose Yourself</a>" with Carson sitting in the audience. After the song, Carson took the stage and announced his candidacy alongside a speech on his <a href="/wiki/Rags_to_riches" title="Rags to riches">rags to riches</a> life story, at one point stating: "I remember when our favorite drug dealer was killed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surge_in_polls">Surge in polls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ben_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Surge in polls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_9.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3389" data-file-width="5084" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_9.jpg/220px-Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_9.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_9.jpg/330px-Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_9.jpg/440px-Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_9.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_9.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carson taking a photo with a supporter at a rally in August 2015</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In October 2015, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super_PAC" title="Super PAC">Super PAC</a> supporting Carson, The 2016 Committee (formerly the Draft Carson Committee) announced it had received donations in mostly $100 increments from more than 200 small businesses around the country over the course of one week. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fox_Business" title="Fox Business">Fox Business</a></i> reported that "Carson's outsider status is growing his small business support base." Ben Walters, a fundraiser for The 2016 Committee expressed optimism about Carson's small business support base: "It's unbelievable the diversity of businesses that we are bringing on. We are seeing everything from doctors’ offices and folks in the healthcare profession to motorcycle repair shops and bed and breakfasts."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October, it was noted that Carson's "improbable" political career had surged in polls and fundraising, while he continued to participate in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republican_Party_presidential_debates,_2016" title="Republican Party presidential debates, 2016">nationally-televised Republican debates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_in_polls">Decline in polls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ben_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Decline in polls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_8.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2727" data-file-width="4090" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_8.jpg/220px-Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_8.jpg/330px-Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_8.jpg/440px-Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_8.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_8.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carson speaking before the Nevada caucuses in February 2016</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The campaign brought considerable attention to Carson's past. <a href="/wiki/CBS_News" title="CBS News">CBS News</a> described Carson's narrative of "overcoming impossible odds as a child growing up in an impoverished, single-parent household to reach international prominence as a pediatric neurosurgeon" as "a key part of his presidential campaign." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> said the narrative came under "the harsh scrutiny of presidential politics, where rivals and media hunt for embellishments and omissions that can hobble a campaign."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> CNN characterized the core narrative as "acts of violence as an angry young man," followed by a spiritual epiphany that transformed Carson into the "composed figure" he now portrays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tale_of_two_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-tale_of_two-80">[80]</a></sup> Media challenges to a number of Carson's statements included allegations of discrepancies between documented facts and certain assertions in his autobiography <i>Gifted Hands</i>—allegations dismissed by Carson as a media "witch hunt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witch_hunt_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-witch_hunt-83">[83]</a></sup> In November 2015, the <i><a href="/wiki/Detroit_Free_Press" title="Detroit Free Press">Detroit Free Press</a></i> republished an article from 1988 "to try to bring some clarity to the claims currently being brought into question."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88freepress_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-88freepress-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 2015, Carson's campaign aired a 60-second TV advertisement in which excerpts from Carson's stump speech were intercut with a rap by an artist named Aspiring Mogul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RapAd_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-RapAd-158">[158]</a></sup> They spent $150,000 on the ads, which were aired in Atlanta, Detroit and Miami.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Carson initially weakly defended the ad, saying "Well, there are people in the campaign who felt that was a good way to do things ... I support them in doing that, but I probably would have taken a little different approach."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RapAd_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-RapAd-158">[158]</a></sup> He then later said the advertisement was done without his knowledge, that "it was done by people who have no concept of the black community and what they were doing", and that he was "horrified" by it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horrified_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horrified-160">[160]</a></sup> A reporter pointed out that the advertisement contained an explicit approval statement from Carson, and he replied "Well, obviously. But you notice no more of those kind of ads coming out now."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horrified_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-Horrified-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>Statements that Carson made regarding foreign policy called into doubt his familiarity with the domain. <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> reported in 2015, "Carson has acknowledged being something of a novice on foreign affairs". Regarding the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_crisis" title="Ukrainian crisis">Ukrainian crisis</a>, Carson would send arms to Ukraine to aid it in its fight against pro-Russian rebels. He also believes the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic states</a> should "get involved in NATO" (apparently unaware that they are NATO members).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a November 2015 Republican debate, Carson declared his intentions to make <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">ISIS</a> "look like losers" as he would "destroy their caliphate". Carson also advocated capturing a "big energy field" outside of <a href="/wiki/Anbar_(town)" title="Anbar (town)">Anbar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, which he said could be accomplished "fairly easily". Regarding the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, he also claimed that "the Chinese are there"; while in contrast, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> reported that "there are no known members of the Chinese armed forces currently engaged in any conflict in the Middle East".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISlosers_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISlosers-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carson said that he is not opposed to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palestinian_state" title="Palestinian state">Palestinian state</a>, but questioned why it needs "to be within the confines of Israeli territory […] Is that necessary, or can you sort of slip that area down into Egypt?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BG.greatest-hits_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-BG.greatest-hits-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Withdrawal_from_campaign">Withdrawal from campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ben_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Withdrawal from campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On March 2, following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super_Tuesday,_2016" title="Super Tuesday, 2016">Super Tuesday, 2016</a> primaries, Carson announced that he did "not see a political path forward" and would not attend the next Republican debate in Detroit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTerrisWashPst03022016_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-BTerrisWashPst03022016-15">[15]</a></sup> He said, "this grassroots movement on behalf of 'We the People' will continue," indicating that he would give more details later in the week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTerrisWashPst03022016_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-BTerrisWashPst03022016-15">[15]</a></sup> He suspended his campaign on March 4 and announced he would be the new national chairman of My Faith Votes, a group that encourages Christians to exercise their civic duty to vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTerrisWashPst03022016_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-BTerrisWashPst03022016-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TKopanCNN03042016_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-TKopanCNN03042016-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>In total, Ben Carson's campaign spent $58 million. However, most of the money went to political consultants and fundraising rather than advertising. Carson questioned whether his campaign was economically sabotaged from within.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_activities_during_2016_election">Further activities during 2016 election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ben_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Further activities during 2016 election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On March 11, 2016, a week after Carson ended his presidential campaign, he endorsed Trump, calling him part of "the voice of the people to be heard."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Carson's subsequent comments that Americans would only have to sustain Trump for four years if he was not a good president drew criticism and he admitted that he would have preferred another candidate though thought Trump had the best chance of winning the general election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> On the other hand, at the press conference Carson stated that Trump had a "cerebral" side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-washingtonpost.com_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-washingtonpost.com-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 16, Carson spoke favorably of the possibility of abolishing the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a>, believing it disregards "the will of the people".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> Later that month, on April 25, Carson expressed opposition to <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Tubman" title="Harriet Tubman">Harriet Tubman</a> replacing <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_twenty-dollar_bill" title="United States twenty-dollar bill">$20 bill</a>, the day after dubbing the replacement "political expediency", though he indicated interest in Tubman having another tribute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> In late April, Carson wrote to the <a href="/wiki/Nevada_Republican_Party" title="Nevada Republican Party">Nevada Republican Party</a>, requesting the two delegates he won in <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a> be released and free to support whoever they want.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>On May 4, after Trump wrapped up the Republican nomination, he hinted that Carson would be among those who would vet his vice-presidential pick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> The same day, Carson in an interview expressed interest in <a href="/wiki/Ted_Cruz" title="Ted Cruz">Ted Cruz</a> serving as either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attorney_General_of_the_United_States" title="Attorney General of the United States">Attorney General of the United States</a>, a position that Carson said would allow Cruz to prosecute <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a>, or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_Justice" title="Supreme Court Justice">Supreme Court Justice</a> in a Trump administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> On May 6, Carson said in interview that Trump would consider a Democrat as his running mate, conflicting with Trump's asserting that he would not. A Carson spokesperson later said Carson expected Trump to select a Republican.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Carson was said by aide Armstrong Williams in a May 10 interview to have withdrawn from the Trump campaign's vetting team, though the campaign confirmed he was still involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> Later that month, Carson revealed a list of potential vice-presidential candidates in an interview with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_Post" title="Washington Post">Washington Post</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> On May 16, Carson said the media could not keep opinion out of reporting and cited <a href="/wiki/Walter_Cronkite" title="Walter Cronkite">Walter Cronkite</a> as a fair journalist that was in his words a "left-wing radical."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
